  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ee5242f6-1983-4c87-8f64-aedaa444b575'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer18221152125470432871">Dans l'immense paysage gelé, les membres des Expéditions Polaires françaises font un relevé du relief sous-glaciaire. Un incroyable phénomène se produit : les appareils sondeurs enregistrent un signal. Il y a un émetteur sous la glace...<br/><br/>Que vont découvrir les savants et les techniciens venus du monde entier qui creusent la glace à la rencontre du mystère ?<br/><br/><i>La Nuit des temps</i></span>
  <span id="freeText18221152125470432871" style="display:none">Dans l'immense paysage gelé, les membres des Expéditions Polaires françaises font un relevé du relief sous-glaciaire. Un incroyable phénomène se produit : les appareils sondeurs enregistrent un signal. Il y a un émetteur sous la glace...<br /><br />Que vont découvrir les savants et les techniciens venus du monde entier qui creusent la glace à la rencontre du mystère ?<br /><br /><i>La Nuit des temps</i>, c'est à la fois un reportage, une épopée mêlant présent et futur, et un rand chant d'amour passionné. Traversant le laine universel comme un trait de feu, le destin d'Eléa et de Païkan les emmène vers le grand mythe des amants légendaires.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7456810842784836512">Simply the best SciFi romance I have ever read. This would make a great movie. It has science, history, romance, politics, media skullduggery...<br/>The setting is only a little bit dated and could easily be updated current events because the story is timeless. <br/>Plot summary:<br/>An international expedition finds a lost world underneath the Antarctic ice and discovers two people frozen at absolute zero perched on a pedastal in a perfect golden egg. The woman in the egg is in better physical condition so sh</span>
  <span id="freeText7456810842784836512" style="display:none">Simply the best SciFi romance I have ever read. This would make a great movie. It has science, history, romance, politics, media skullduggery...<br />The setting is only a little bit dated and could easily be updated current events because the story is timeless. <br />Plot summary:<br />An international expedition finds a lost world underneath the Antarctic ice and discovers two people frozen at absolute zero perched on a pedastal in a perfect golden egg. The woman in the egg is in better physical condition so she is revived first. She tells a tale of a nation forced underground by perpetual war, of machines that can make food out of pure energy and of her perfect companion, chosen for her when she was a child. While images of her lost world are broadcast, people take to the street in protest of their political and economic conditions and the scientist who works so hard to bring her back to life watches her slip away into memory and the night of time. <br />Not to be missed. Oh, wait! It's out of print. Drat. <br />I found a copy at a used bookstore. Worth every penny.</span>

It had a decent premise, two people from an advanced civilization that disappeared 900,000 years ago are found frozen in Antarctica and revived. The book was okay and a decent read but not worthy of inclusion on a "best" list (in my opinion at least).</span>

<div id="review_1168720024" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1168720024" />
    <a title="Tzu-Mainn Chen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29853114-tzu-mainn-chen"><img alt="Tzu-Mainn Chen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1168720024?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tzu-Mainn Chen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tzu-Mainn Chen" href="/user/show/29853114-tzu-mainn-chen">Tzu-Mainn Chen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/11645678-the-ice-people">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1168720024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7396119097548982776">This is a confusing book to rate. There is much in "The Ice People" that is not good. The characters feel like mouthpieces. The plot is more convenient than organic. And yet I can't deny that there is something strangely unsettling about the book, and not necessarily in a bad way.<br/><br/>"The Ice People" (translated from the French "La Nuit des Temps") tells the story of an international coalition of scientists and their incredible discovery far beneath the Antarctic ice: the last remnants of a highly a</span>
  <span id="freeText7396119097548982776" style="display:none">This is a confusing book to rate. There is much in "The Ice People" that is not good. The characters feel like mouthpieces. The plot is more convenient than organic. And yet I can't deny that there is something strangely unsettling about the book, and not necessarily in a bad way.<br /><br />"The Ice People" (translated from the French "La Nuit des Temps") tells the story of an international coalition of scientists and their incredible discovery far beneath the Antarctic ice: the last remnants of a highly advanced 900,000 year old human civilization, including two cryogenically preserved survivors. As the scientists struggle to communicate with the survivors and glean the wondrous knowledge that they possess, the outside world is caught between awe and terror of what that new technology might mean for them.<br /><br />Barjavel grew up with the First World War and witnessed the Second. When he wrote "The Ice People" in 1968, the world was caught in the deep shadows of the Cold War (which was a far more frightening time than can be understood through history books). "The Ice People" feels like a passionate response to a world gone mad, a breath away from complete and utter destruction. Every conflict is of the highest stakes; characters spout idealistic screeds extolling the desperate measures needed to save mankind.<br /><br />None of that makes for great reading, and yet... there is something about the author's passion that is deeply affecting. He describes technological wonders that can be accomplished through the putting aside of differences (a global cloud computing network, a universal translator), and those descriptions are moving in a way that similar descriptions in modern science fiction books are not. In addition, the overt idealism in 'The Ice People' grated on me, and yet also let me strangely wistful. That reaction left me wondering if there is a part of me that wishes for something that would wash my adult cynicism away, so that I could wholeheartedly and unreservedly believe in the ultimate salvation of mankind.<br /><br />And then there is the love story that lies at the heart of "The Ice People". Unlike the other plot threads, the tale of Elea and Paikan is human, well told, and could have sustained a book all by itself. It too left me wistful, but for the belief that true love is attainable and within reach.<br /><br />Modern books are nuanced and subtle and full of greys, and I love them for that. But I guess what "The Ice People" taught me is that I miss the days when I believed that the world could be pure and perfect.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7396119097548982776" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2126021021" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2126021021" />
    <a title="Justine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71147186-justine"><img alt="Justine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514980922p2/71147186.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2126021021?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justine" href="/user/show/71147186-justine">Justine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782266000482" href="/book/show/970055.La_Nuit_des_temps">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71147186-justine?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2126021021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15432820736763756310">4.5-5<br/><br/>First, this book can be read super quickly. Honestly, if I had nothing to do laast weekend, and if I didn't want to take my time to read the end, it would have been a really quick read. The writing is easy to understand and transport the reader in the South Pole, where the story is set. <br/><br/>I was not charmed by the beginning of the book: the setting was not attractive to me, and some things bugged me, like sexism and a certain way to speak of people of colour. I tried to go beyond that to appre</span>
  <span id="freeText15432820736763756310" style="display:none">4.5-5<br /><br />First, this book can be read super quickly. Honestly, if I had nothing to do laast weekend, and if I didn't want to take my time to read the end, it would have been a really quick read. The writing is easy to understand and transport the reader in the South Pole, where the story is set. <br /><br />I was not charmed by the beginning of the book: the setting was not attractive to me, and some things bugged me, like sexism and a certain way to speak of people of colour. I tried to go beyond that to appreciate the story itself.<br /><br />I don't exactly know when I fell in love, but I did. <br /><br />-I grew to love the setting, its beauty, and what it reveals. It felt like I could feel the cold!! <br /><br />-I loved Elea and Païkan's story! I usually don't like romance; but here, it didn't bother me, quite the contrary! I understood them, and loved to see their bonds, the way they feel, the way they are one. "Love" is not a sufficient word to get us to understand what they feel for one another. We sometimes feel that too when we're in love: three words can't summarize everything we are to each other. The scenes telling their story were my favorites, because we get to know them, but also because we get to know their world! <br /><br />-I adored discovering this lost civilisation, it was fascinating, beautiful, and also quite sad. There is a reflexion here about us humans: we can't think bigger than ourselves for the most part. We only think about territories, possessions; we're capable to wreck the earth, just because we don't want the other to have it. War, such an idiocy. Gondawa seems idyllic: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[no ideologies, no religions, no real money. People, thanks to their personal key, can have whatever they want, whenever they want it. They have credits on the key, enough for basic needs, but also for leisure! Sexuality is not a shame, work is not a burden, having children can't be accidental, people live in harmony with nature and get everything they need from universal energy; men and women seem to be equal. Such a Heaven ... <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />-I also loved Coban's character (even if we don't get to know him that much) and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[think that, with his death, it's love that wins, despite everything. Païkan succeeds in following Elea, and they might have lived 900 000 years after their civilisation died; unfortunately, she thinks he is Coban, and not her loved one. Their death is a tragedy, quite like Romeo and Juliet's!! It might be deemed a retelling after all! Love is more important than knowledge and civilisation. I don't really know what I feel about it; I can understand both view points.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />-I was quite shocked by the ending!!! <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Everything that we read was annihilated! We might think: "everything, just for that?!! Just for this failure?!!" The Gondawa civilisation is lost once more, with all its knowledge - or is it? The last scene, with the son of the Vignonts, might mean that there is still something to do to save them, and their world and knowledge. Plus, Zoran's Equation has been saved for someone: who has it? where is it? can they make it work?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />So, it was a great read, and my first Barjavel! Can't wait to read more! </span>
  <a data-text-id="15432820736763756310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_394773967" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/394773967" />
    <a title="Angélique Moreau" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11991749-ang-lique-moreau"><img alt="Angélique Moreau" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499810542p2/11991749.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/394773967?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angélique Moreau" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angélique Moreau" href="/user/show/11991749-ang-lique-moreau">Angélique Moreau</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11991749-ang-lique-moreau?shelf=utopia-dystopia">utopia-dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer394773967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12969283849556645081"><i>La Nuit des Temps</i> is a love story, but also a dystopia that reveals a world long buried for thousands of years under the ice.<br/><br/>I read it as a child and was lucky enough to study it in junior high during our cycle on fantasy and horror literature.<br/><br/>Of course, this novel bears the mark of Barjavel's style, of his hippie way of approching the world, in the style of 1968, but full of hopes about humanity's managing to improve.<br/><br/>There's a culture-shock between modern humans and this woman melted out of her</span>
  <span id="freeText12969283849556645081" style="display:none"><i>La Nuit des Temps</i> is a love story, but also a dystopia that reveals a world long buried for thousands of years under the ice.<br /><br />I read it as a child and was lucky enough to study it in junior high during our cycle on fantasy and horror literature.<br /><br />Of course, this novel bears the mark of Barjavel's style, of his hippie way of approching the world, in the style of 1968, but full of hopes about humanity's managing to improve.<br /><br />There's a culture-shock between modern humans and this woman melted out of her block of ice, who tells of her perfect world, which nevertheless counts a great deal of excluded people and is after all a computerised society in which everybody has a number (here, a ring that activates the machines, doors and lifts necessary to circulate, fonction in society and even get food and informations).<br /><br />If Eléa's feelings for Païkan are universal, they do not conceal the fact that this book is a piece of social criticism.<br /><br /><br />^v^v^v^v^v^v^v^v^v^v^v^v^v^v^<br /><br /><i>La Nuit des Temps</i> est un roman d'amour, mais aussi une dystopie qui nous présente un monde disparu sous les glaces depuis des milliers d'années.<br /><br />Je l'avais lu quand j'étais enfant et avait eu le plaisir de l'étudier au collège pendant notre cycle sur la littérature de fantaisie et d'horreur. <br /><br />Bien entendu, le roman porte la marque de Barjavel, sa façon un peu hippie de voir le monde, très soixante-huitarde mais pleine d'espoir sur le fait que les humains sauront toujours s'en sortir.<br /><br />C'est le choc des cultures entre les humains modernes et cette femme sortie de son bloc de glace, qui raconte son monde parfait, mais qui compte tout de même son nombre d'exclus et est après tout une société informatisée dans laquelle chacun a un numéro (ici, une bague, qui lui permet d'activer les différentes machines, portes et ascenseurs nécessaires aux déplacements, au fonctionnement dans la société et à l’accès a la nourriture et aux informations).<br /><br />Si les sentiments d'Eléa pour Païkan sont universels, ils ne masquent pas le fait que le livre est une critique sociale.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12969283849556645081" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_614091887" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/614091887" />
    <a title="Charlotte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/327206-charlotte"><img alt="Charlotte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1191783784p2/327206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/614091887?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlotte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlotte" href="/user/show/327206-charlotte">Charlotte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780688018603" href="/book/show/106750.The_Ice_People">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer614091887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18079272180952509309">This novel seems to be slightly obscure in the English-language sci-fi world (the original French version was a best-seller after it came out in France in 1970), which is surprising, because it's a lot of fun: there's fast-moving action, romance, idealistic scientists trying to save humanity from the machinations of cynical politicians, and all the far-fetched futuristic technology that we hope for when we read sci-fi novels. It would surely be embraced by international sci-fi fans if it came to</span>
  <span id="freeText18079272180952509309" style="display:none">This novel seems to be slightly obscure in the English-language sci-fi world (the original French version was a best-seller after it came out in France in 1970), which is surprising, because it's a lot of fun: there's fast-moving action, romance, idealistic scientists trying to save humanity from the machinations of cynical politicians, and all the far-fetched futuristic technology that we hope for when we read sci-fi novels. It would surely be embraced by international sci-fi fans if it came to be better known. If you like sci-fi, go out and find this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18079272180952509309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1659259678" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1659259678" />
    <a title="Nina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23826312-nina"><img alt="Nina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378825390p2/23826312.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1659259678?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nina" href="/user/show/23826312-nina">Nina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nina by: </span>
      Jennifer Frisanco
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23826312-nina?shelf=sci-fi-alt-history">sci-fi-alt-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23826312-nina?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1659259678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7308388781011472947">Early sci fi, which unfortunately hasn't aged well. <br/><br/>There are several original, intriguing ideas, such as: the ability of science and discovery to cross geo-political boundaries (especially at times of heightened tensions when little else can); the devastating loss of irreplaceable artifacts that can arise when science rushes forward with insufficient precautions; the limits of cooperative governance structures; and the devastating effects of geopolitical game theory. It was pretty cool to read </span>
  <span id="freeText7308388781011472947" style="display:none">Early sci fi, which unfortunately hasn't aged well. <br /><br />There are several original, intriguing ideas, such as: the ability of science and discovery to cross geo-political boundaries (especially at times of heightened tensions when little else can); the devastating loss of irreplaceable artifacts that can arise when science rushes forward with insufficient precautions; the limits of cooperative governance structures; and the devastating effects of geopolitical game theory. It was pretty cool to read one of the earliest sci fi imaginings of big data computer modeling. The book is definitely rooted in the 1960s: the narrowly averted Cuban Missile Crisis, the limits of Mutually Assured Destruction, growing popular/youth/utopian unrest, increasing investment in science/tech dominance, the role of the media in bolstering popular support for scientific investment and the resulting push to turn the process of scientific discovery into a digestible reality TV style performance. And this was written before the moon landing and before the 1968 student riots! Impressively prescient. <br /><br />However, the 1960s also left their mark in less desirable ways. There are extremely uncomfortable racist and mysogynist moments, somewhat akin to the "Tintin au Congo" comic book or the "Little Black Sambo" children's book. People are described as black, yellow, red, etc. Women are described first and foremost by their physical attributes, the Russian scientist is described as something of a harpy who regularly gets slapped on the ass, and men take the lead on everything professional/scientific even when the women have literally the exact same training (which the author does not seem to have a problem with). The narrator is supposed to be this endearing example of unrequited love who takes care of Elea when everyone else treats her as a specimen, but I couldn't get over how he fell for her upon seen her "perfect naked body" and his vision of love seemed awfully close to a mildly manipulative yearning to posses her in body and soul. Excuse me while I go throw up. <br /><br />Not to mention that I found the core love story to be over the top, eyeroll-inducing, saccharine shlok. No joke, these are perfect physical specimens of the human race, with the perfect amorous relationship, riding bareback through the forest, strolling along a lake (while allowing the horses to disappear god knows where), and then having perfect sex in a swimming pool. All that was missing was puppies and rainbows. The perfect characters and their perfect lives were reminiscent of Barbie and Ken dolls - plastic, unrelatable, and dull. The world-building and the plot itself were also pretty shallow and unbelievable. <br /><br />Overall, 1960s mores aside, I think I would have enjoyed it much more had I read it as a young teen.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7308388781011472947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3116199575" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3116199575" />
    <a title="Astrid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26915491-astrid"><img alt="Astrid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1429675965p2/26915491.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3116199575?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Astrid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Astrid" href="/user/show/26915491-astrid">Astrid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26915491-astrid?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26915491-astrid?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3116199575" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3910250642809719948">I wasn't going to rate this, originally, because it's slightly unfair to review a classic by today's standards. I think it's tricky to read books from the 20th century because the language is very much not dated whilst the discourse is. Plus sci-fi was only getting started at the time, so things that feel tropey were just the beginning of a genre.<br/><br/>And here we are. <br/><br/>In the end it bugged me too much. This is a book many French people read as young teens, and reading it as an adult in 2020 stings. It</span>
  <span id="freeText3910250642809719948" style="display:none">I wasn't going to rate this, originally, because it's slightly unfair to review a classic by today's standards. I think it's tricky to read books from the 20th century because the language is very much not dated whilst the discourse is. Plus sci-fi was only getting started at the time, so things that feel tropey were just the beginning of a genre.<br /><br />And here we are. <br /><br />In the end it bugged me too much. This is a book many French people read as young teens, and reading it as an adult in 2020 stings. It's very misogynistic and racist, and the erotic scenes that were certainly rebelious at the time are eye roll inducing now.<br /><br />The story is ok, didn't grab me but no major flaws, the ending is alright as well. Didn't care for or believe in either of the romances, described in almost biblical terms of one perfect woman joining with one perfect man (blong, blue eyed) since their childhood and unable to live without one another. The other world that hosts part of the story bored me to death, everything was golden and beautiful and peaceful and magical, please. <br /><br />Everyone says this book is well written but the language didn't work for me at all. The first part was very flat. The second part had all these lyrical, poetic moments but described with the same ten words over and over again. It didn't feel mastered, it felt like a 20 year old boy wrote it. (it's also so straight, and so phallocentric) <br /><br />Really the only good parts in this book were the scenes that didn't involve any main characters, and focused instead on how society reacted to the plot. And I chuckled a couple of times at the humor.<br /><br />Also they invented the internet! That was fun. And the student riot got a mention.<br /><br />Anwyay. If you haven't read this as a kid don't bother.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3910250642809719948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1420074525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5554523585741584768">This is my all time favorite book. I read it for the first time 22 years ago. I was eight, I was bored, the library was closed for the holidays and it was there in my sister's room, waiting for me. I was too young to understand every word but the sheer beauty and emotion of the love story just blew me away. I've read it every year since. I grew up with it, I understood it better. Why is it my favourite? That's simple: I know every word and still I can't help holding my breath as I keep reading, </span>
  <span id="freeText5554523585741584768" style="display:none">This is my all time favorite book. I read it for the first time 22 years ago. I was eight, I was bored, the library was closed for the holidays and it was there in my sister's room, waiting for me. I was too young to understand every word but the sheer beauty and emotion of the love story just blew me away. I've read it every year since. I grew up with it, I understood it better. Why is it my favourite? That's simple: I know every word and still I can't help holding my breath as I keep reading, hoping maybe that with this new reading, things might change, that maybe I would not cry my eyes off. But I still do and I love it all the same.<br />This is just my experience, my opinion. Some might think otherwise but still, I recommend it.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3194565849431356972">This novel is about the discovery of an ancient, advanced civilization under the Antarctic ice sheet. This may be due to the translation. It was originally in French andt did win the <i>Prix des Libraires</i> for 1969. At least it was short.</span>

<div id="review_2356074447" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2356074447" />
    <a title="Terri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14703126-terri"><img alt="Terri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371907883p2/14703126.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2356074447?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terri" href="/user/show/14703126-terri">Terri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782258092327" href="/book/show/18306842-la-nuit-des-temps">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14703126-terri?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2356074447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6565063123373170877">La Nuit des Temps (published in English as The Ice People) is a massively exciting 1960s sci-fi novel about a French scientific expedition who discover the traces of a lost civilisation deep under the Antarctic ice. And not only that but, impossibly, after 900,000 years, someone is alive down there...<br/><br/>The story of what becomes an international team trying to unravel the mystery has a brilliant sense of optimism and faith in scientific discovery. Barjavel is great at giving his tale a worldwide sc</span>
  <span id="freeText6565063123373170877" style="display:none">La Nuit des Temps (published in English as The Ice People) is a massively exciting 1960s sci-fi novel about a French scientific expedition who discover the traces of a lost civilisation deep under the Antarctic ice. And not only that but, impossibly, after 900,000 years, someone is alive down there...<br /><br />The story of what becomes an international team trying to unravel the mystery has a brilliant sense of optimism and faith in scientific discovery. Barjavel is great at giving his tale a worldwide scope: quick snapshots of people hearing about the scientists' discoveries in Japan and being vox popped about them in Paris add to the growing sense of excitement. As a reader, I was on tenterhooks to find out wtf was about to be discovered, just as much as the central characters were.<br /><br />The flashbacks to the lost civilisation which start mid-book move skillfully from showing a seeming utopia, to a looming sense of horror and despair. The book is wonderfully cinematic and refreshingly unpredictable. <br /><br />However, oh boy, have attitudes to gender changed over the intervening fifty years since this was published. The way Eléa, the central female character, is portrayed - beautiful, fragile, emotion-driven, entirely lacking in agency - is pretty hard to get past. One of the male characters 'falls in love' with her, but it just reads like irrational, creepy obsession. She also spends a lot of time nude, and there's an unfortunate focus on descriptions of her perfect breasts, etc etc. <br /><br />I encourage anyone who likes sci-fi to read this, but definitely be prepared to grit your teeth through bits of it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6565063123373170877" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3387294600960628497">Dated, but creative. Science fiction from 1968 with all the giddy thrall of possibilities of technology. Buried inside the narrative with its cold-war politics and stodgy notions of gender is a shakespearean tragedy, not fully revealed until the final pages. <br/><br/>The texture is rich if a bit uneven.   <span id="freeText3387294600960628497" style="display:none">Dated, but creative. Science fiction from 1968 with all the giddy thrall of possibilities of technology. Buried inside the narrative with its cold-war politics and stodgy notions of gender is a shakespearean tragedy, not fully revealed until the final pages. <br /><br />The texture is rich if a bit uneven. It's generally an easy if intense read. Though many aspects don't stand up to rigorous scrutiny, every section has something new to offer. The futuristic world of the past is perhaps the most science-fictiony.... it's odd to see what they though life nowadays might be like in 1968. For example, video recording seems to be nonexistent. Yet they can read minds to project the images on a screen! <br /><br />Plus it ends with big explosions, so how can you go wrong? <br /></span>

    <a title="Benoît" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8593248-beno-t"><img alt="Benoît" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1538568913p2/8593248.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2025191744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13654236995436034095">I could see this being a good comic, a good illustrator could do wonders, and the story could pass. As a novel, it's flawed, close to pulp fiction at times, and Barjavel can be annoying, arrogant, and stupid. His vision of women is a typical mixture of cheesy idealization and casual misogyny that I find nauseating and cannot understand anymore. The "international" team of scientists falls short of what it should be, with little intelligence and underdeveloped characters, and with the seemingly a</span>
  <span id="freeText13654236995436034095" style="display:none">I could see this being a good comic, a good illustrator could do wonders, and the story could pass. As a novel, it's flawed, close to pulp fiction at times, and Barjavel can be annoying, arrogant, and stupid. His vision of women is a typical mixture of cheesy idealization and casual misogyny that I find nauseating and cannot understand anymore. The "international" team of scientists falls short of what it should be, with little intelligence and underdeveloped characters, and with the seemingly autobiographical Simon who shows very disturbing behavior in the name of "love" for the main character, Elea, the perfect girl that has been preserved through all the ages. Not much happens until they find her, then there's a rather interesting middle part where we discover stuff. And then a long ending, where Barjavel pictures a dream society made of abundance, beauty and perfect love which is paradoxically engulfed by violence and seriously looks like a nightmare in the end. Maybe he was trying to be ironic, but I found it unsatisfactory.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13654236995436034095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2582677556" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2582677556" />
    <a title="Alex Orion" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21938425-alex-orion"><img alt="Alex Orion" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/images/nophoto/user/c_111x148.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2582677556?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex Orion" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex Orion" href="/user/show/21938425-alex-orion">Alex Orion</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21938425-alex-orion?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2582677556&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2582677556" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3291818068210307019">This is the first novel I've read in French in its entirety, and that's because I didn't want to stop reading - I had to know what happened next! It's an exciting, fast paced story that kept me in suspense from the very beginning, and with twists and action I never saw coming.<br/>I think good scifi, at its heart, shows the truth about humans as we are, and the potential of what we can be. La Nuit Des Temps absolutely does that: our curiosity through science, the power we can have when we come togeth</span>
  <span id="freeText3291818068210307019" style="display:none">This is the first novel I've read in French in its entirety, and that's because I didn't want to stop reading - I had to know what happened next! It's an exciting, fast paced story that kept me in suspense from the very beginning, and with twists and action I never saw coming.<br />I think good scifi, at its heart, shows the truth about humans as we are, and the potential of what we can be. La Nuit Des Temps absolutely does that: our curiosity through science, the power we can have when we come together across borders, our simultaneous fear and longing for the unknown, and the unpleasantly real tendency to turn to distrust and violence when we don't understand each other or when things go unexpectedly.<br />I like the way it's structured - hearing Simon's thoughts after the fact keep the tension up throughout the novel, and the news interviews, snapshots of the family and other breaks from the events of the South Pole broadens the story to include the whole modern world.<br />However, there are obvious aspects that didn't age well. Having an obnoxiously sexist character doesn't absolve the main character's own possessiveness and objectification of Eléa, just because it's less overt. The idea that all Black people come from Mars, and most brown people are descendants of the greedy, chaotic, lazy society, where the Perfect Utopic Gondawa (though I enjoyed the exploration of the cracks in that country) is populated entirely by white people, is just racist.<br /><br />Overall: excellent story with flaws, much better when you picture the star-crossed lovers, Eléa and Païkan, as Janelle Monae and Tessa Thompson in your head.<br /><br />Favourite line: La poussière du sol avalait le bruit des pas, celle des murs le bruit des paroles.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3291818068210307019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2810884019" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2810884019" />
    <a title="Christopher Miller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/88087493-christopher-miller"><img alt="Christopher Miller" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2810884019?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher Miller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher Miller" href="/user/show/88087493-christopher-miller">Christopher Miller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780688018603" href="/book/show/106750.The_Ice_People">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2810884019" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7575523648134842328">This book had its flaws, but I really enjoyed reading it. The message is delivered in a pretty heavy-handed manner. It seems that way, anyway. The society that the frozen female Elea is at first meant to be portrayed as utopian. However, as we spend more time there other aspects that say otherwise are revealed and I don't know if that was intentional or if the author needed to invent them to further the plot. <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[Suddenly, in this peaceful society there's a vast underground army that </span></span>
  <span id="freeText7575523648134842328" style="display:none">This book had its flaws, but I really enjoyed reading it. The message is delivered in a pretty heavy-handed manner. It seems that way, anyway. The society that the frozen female Elea is at first meant to be portrayed as utopian. However, as we spend more time there other aspects that say otherwise are revealed and I don't know if that was intentional or if the author needed to invent them to further the plot. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Suddenly, in this peaceful society there's a vast underground army that only emerges when things get crazy. A haste-flavored backstory is given to them just as they're introduced. Suddenly, there's a social pariah who has no key (equivalent to a credit card) who survives on scraps offered by others, in a society where there is no scarcity and no one is required to even work. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />The scenes in the distant past were the slower part of the book for me. There was still an us versus them, right way/wrong way overtone, and again I don't know if it came from the author's beliefs or if he was actually meaning to portray that some things never change. If the latter, it was so well done it will take a few days to fully sink in.<br />What I enjoyed most was the science fiction aspects from the "present" (1970s). I enjoyed the excavation and revival scenes; even knowing what the premise was, they had a page-turning feeling of mystery for me. The ancient technology was closer to fantasy, but can serve as food for thought.<br />I'd recommend reading this, especially for a book club. It has enough in it to take on additional life when followed by discussion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7575523648134842328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2647939723" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2647939723" />
    <a title="Kathleen Boylan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73149331-kathleen-boylan"><img alt="Kathleen Boylan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509290430p2/73149331.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2647939723?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen Boylan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen Boylan" href="/user/show/73149331-kathleen-boylan">Kathleen Boylan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2647939723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16921278011843890853">I remember first reading this book at age 12 and absolutely loving it. I found a used copy recently and I wanted to see if the story held up over time. It mostly does. The plot uses a frame device where scientists exploring the Antarctic stumble upon a signal being beamed from miles down in the ice, and they find buried there a compound where a man and a woman have been frozen and perfectly preserved at absolute zero. The woman is revived first, and we then have the crux of the story - she tells</span>
  <span id="freeText16921278011843890853" style="display:none">I remember first reading this book at age 12 and absolutely loving it. I found a used copy recently and I wanted to see if the story held up over time. It mostly does. The plot uses a frame device where scientists exploring the Antarctic stumble upon a signal being beamed from miles down in the ice, and they find buried there a compound where a man and a woman have been frozen and perfectly preserved at absolute zero. The woman is revived first, and we then have the crux of the story - she tells the story of her civilization, how it was destroyed, and we learn of her love for Paikan (her husband for lack of a better word). <br /><br />The notion of an advanced ancient civilization that destroyed itself through its technology with some sort of "solar weapon" so powerful that it tilted the Earth's axis is an interesting premise, and Barjavel clearly has a moral message about the folly of war as well as the importance of living n balance with nature. I recommend the book for people who like science fiction. <br /><br /><br /><br />(published as The Ice People in English, and out of print)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16921278011843890853" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText17808003252932405222" style="display:none">I read this book in its original language (French), so I don't know what the English version is like. That's definitely on my to-do list! It's unusual to find sci-fi written in a language that is not English (other than Jules Verne), yet it is really worth it if you are looking for something new. <br />This book reminds me of Planet of the Apes or Star Wars The Empire Strikes Back, in the sense that you get a big surprise. Once you know the revelation/secret, there's no going back, you can't re-watch and say: what was that big revelation again? Same with this book. The ending is so poignant you carry it with you for a long time.<br />This is a forgotten sci-fi novel that should be re-discovered.<br />(side note: did you know Planet of the Apes was originally written by a French author?)</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cS5HKXs74ZJ+QDYn1HcISNx7PBox5t4KNOgBAiYhTMY8SzzWwf85b++9dCpCN4Us6yQUtEsNdHn0GuY+cDSTzA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Unique, daring, and unforgettable, it tells the story of an ordinary family who accidentally threaten the security of a hidden civilization as intelligent as our own--a colony of ants determined to survive at any cost....Jonathan Wells and his young family have come to the Paris flat at 3, rue des Sybarites through the bequest of his eccentric late uncle Edmond. Inheriting the dusty apartment, the Wells family are left with only one warning: Never go down into the cellar.But when the family dog disappears down the basement steps, Jonathan follows--and soon his wife, his son, and various would-be rescuers vanish into its mysterious depths.Meanwhile, in a pine stump in a nearby park, a vast civilization is in turmoil. Here a young female from the russet ant nation of Bel-o-kan learns that a strange new weapon has been killing off her comrades. To find out why, she enlists the help of a warrior ant, and the two set off on separate journeys into a harsh and violent world. It is a world where death takes many forms--savage birds and voracious lizards, warlike dwarf ants and rapacious termites, poisonous beetles and, most bizarre of all, the swift, murderous, giant guardians of the edge of the world: cars.Yet the end of the female\'s desperate quest will be the eerie secret in the cellar at 3, rue des Sybarites--a mystery she must solve in order to fulfill her special destiny as the new queen of her own great empire. But to do so she must first make unthinkable communion with the most barbaric creatures of all. Empire of the Ants is a brilliant evocation of a hidden civilization as complex as our own and far more ancient. It is a fascinating realm where boats are built of leaves and greenflies are domesticated and milked like cows, where citizens lock antennae in &quot;absolute communication&quot; and fight wars with precisely coordinated armies using sprays of glue and acids that can dissolve a snail. Not since Watership Down has a novel so vividly captured the lives and struggles of a fellow species and the valuable lessons they have to teach us.From the Hardcover edition.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/61797.Ren_Barjavel">About René Barjavel</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18095394977508383650">René Barjavel, né le 24 janvier 1911 à Nyons (Drôme) et décédé le 24 novembre 1985 à Paris, est un écrivain et journaliste français principalement connu pour ses romans d'anticipation.<br/><br/>Certains thèmes y reviennent fréquemment : chute de la civilisation causée par les excès de la science et la folie de la guerre, caractère éternel et indestructible de l'amour (Ravage, Le Grand Secret, La Nuit des te</span>
  <span id="freeText18095394977508383650" style="display:none">René Barjavel, né le 24 janvier 1911 à Nyons (Drôme) et décédé le 24 novembre 1985 à Paris, est un écrivain et journaliste français principalement connu pour ses romans d'anticipation.<br /><br />Certains thèmes y reviennent fréquemment : chute de la civilisation causée par les excès de la science et la folie de la guerre, caractère éternel et indestructible de l'amour (Ravage, Le Grand Secret, La Nuit des temps, Une rose au paradis). Son écriture se veut poétique, onirique et, parfois, philosophique. Il a aussi abordé dans de remarquables essais l'interrogation empirique et poétique sur l'existence de Dieu (notamment, La Faim du tigre), et le sens de l'action de l'homme sur la Nature. Il fut aussi scénariste/dialoguiste de films. On lui doit en particulier le scénario du Petit monde de Don Camillo.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18095394977508383650" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
